SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
The project site is generally underlain by soft harbor sediment and volcanic tuff 
overlying stiff alluvium and dense coral formation near Wharf B20. Along the inboard 
alignment of Wharf B20, behind the existing bulkhead, we anticipate the subsurface 
conditions will consist of weathered upper volcanic tuff and coralline deposits overlying stiff 
clays and weathered lower volcanic tuff. At Wharf B21, along the outboard, we anticipate 
the subsurface conditions may consist of a relatively thick layer of loose/soft lagoonal 
deposit overlying stiff alluvium with interbedded layers of weathered volcanic tuff and dense 
coralline material. Along the inboard alignment of Wharf B21, behind the existing bulkhead, 
our field exploration encountered loose/soft lagoonal deposits about 40 to 60 feet thick 
overlying dense coralline material and interbedded layers of stiff alluvium and weathered 
volcanic tuff. 
Based on the design concept and our engineering analyses, we envision that 24-inch 
octagonal precast, prestressed concrete piles may be used to support the proposed new 
wharves with the planned crane rails along the outboard alignment. The 24-inch concrete 
piles at Wharves B20 and B21 would derive vertical support primarily from end-bearing on 
the volcanic tuff formation (mudrock) or from side friction on the dense coralline material. 
Predrilling will be required in order to install the concrete piles to the design tip elevation and 
avoid encountering obstruction anticipated during pile driving operations. 
In order to achieve required vertical compressive and lateral resistance capacities, 
36-inch diameter cast-in-place concrete drilled shafts should be used to support the 
proposed new wharves along the inboard alignment. The drilled shafts would derive vertical 
support primarily from adhesion between the shaft and the surrounding soils. Temporary 
casing may be required to reduce the potential for caving-in of the holes during the drilling 
operation. In addition, placement of concrete by tremie methods will be required for the 
drilled shafts because groundwater will be encountered within the depths of the drilled 
shafts. 
Based on the anticipated subsurface conditions, the fender loads provided, and the 
current design concept, we recommend installing 24-inch square precast, prestressed 
concrete piles at the project site location. The estimated lengths of the concrete fender piles 
would be 80 and 85 feet long for Wharves B20 and B21, respectively. 
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page iv 
Hawaii • California 
To mitigate the potentially liquefiable area in the northern portion of Wharf B21, we 
recommend that overlapping jet-grouted columns arranged in a grid pattern be installed. 
The jet-grouted column grid pattern would confine the liquefiable soils to mitigate the effects 
of liquefaction. Due to the variation of the soft/loose soil thickness, we recommend a probing 
program to be performed prior to the production jet grout column installation, to define the 
final production jet grout column length. In addition, a test jet grouting program is also 
recommended to examine the design parameters of the jet-grout column and the effects of 
the jet grouting mitigation. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussion and specific 
recommendations for the project design. 
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

GEOLABS, INC.

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering evaluation 
performed for the FY22 Special Project RM 17-1007, Repair Wharves B20 & B21 project 
located at Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam in the Honolulu area on the Island of Oahu, 
Hawaii. The project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, 
Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings and geotechnical recommendations resulting 
from our field exploration, laboratory testing, and engineering analyses for the project. 
These findings and geotechnical recommendations are intended for new wharf 
foundations and sheet pile bulkhead only. The findings and recommendations presented 
herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this report. 
1.1 
Project Considerations 
The project site is located at the easternmost end of the Pearl Harbor Naval 
Shipyard along the waterfront in the Pearl Harbor area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The 
existing Bravo Wharves B20 and B21 are approximately 765 feet long and were originally 
constructed in the early 1930s. The foundation system consisting of 18-inch precast 
concrete piles was originally installed to support the concrete wharves along the outboard 
alignment. Continuous strip footings and 18-inch concrete piles were used for Bravo 
Wharves B20 and B21, respectively, along the inboard alignment. A tieback system was 
installed and embedded into the backfill behind the continuous strip footings. 
It should be noted that Wharf B20 was demolished and reconstructed in 2003. The 
wharf originally supported portal cranes, but the reconstruction of the wharf eliminated 
the operation of portal cranes and upgraded the wharf deck to support large mobile crane 
operations only. The existing piles supporting the wharf deck and portal crane rails were 
cut approximately 2 feet above the mudline. New driven pile and drilled shaft foundations 
were installed to support the new deck framing along the outboard and inboard 
alignments, respectively. 
Based on the information provided, the project will involve the demolition and 
reconstruction of the superstructure at the existing Bravo Wharves B20 and B21. The 
SECTION 1. GENERAL 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 2 
Hawaii • California 
objectives of the repair work are to allow for mobile truck crane operations and restore 
portal crane operations with the capacity meeting the requirements of UFC 4-152-01 for 
repair wharves. The collective project will be completed in two phases: Phase 1 will be in 
FY22, and Phase 2 will be in FY23. 
Due to present liquefiable soft/loose soil condition at the northern end of 
Wharf B21, it is desired to mitigate the effects of the liquefaction and reduce potential risk 
of ground settlement that may have extensive impacts to the waterfront operation. 
1.2 
Purpose and Scope 
The purpose of our geotechnical engineering evaluation was to review the surface 
and subsurface conditions to develop an idealized soil and/or rock data set to formulate 
geotechnical engineering recommendations for the design of the project. The work was 
performed in general accordance with our revised fee proposal dated July 22, 2021. The 
scope of work for this exploration included the following tasks and work efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available information and in-house geological 
information in the project vicinity. 
2. 
Procurement of base passes for the field personnel for access to the project 
site. 
3. 
Preparation and submittal of an Accident Prevention Plan with Activity 
Hazard Analysis in support of our field exploration activities and briefing of 
our field personnel of the plan. 
4. 
Preparation and submittal of an application for excavation permit prior to the 
commencement of the field exploration. 
5. 
Site reconnaissance for boring stakeout and access considerations for 
drilling. 
6. 
Utility toning and initiation of Hawaii One Call process for utility clearance 
by our field engineer/geologist. 
7. 
Provision of safety devices around the work area and safety flags for 
vehicles. 
8. 
Provision of a dedicated Site Safety and Health Officer (SSHO) during our 
field exploration work.

SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lavas from the Waianae 
and Koolau shield volcanoes. The older volcano, Waianae, is estimated to be middle to 
late Pliocene in age and forms the bulk of the western one‐third of the island. The younger 
Koolau shield volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene (Ice Age) in 
age and forms the majority of the eastern two‐thirds of the island. The Koolau shield 
reached the end of its main shield‐building phase about 2 million years ago. 
The geologic conditions underlying the project site are the result of a complex 
series of deposition and erosional events brought on by glacio‐eustatic sea level changes 
and post‐erosional volcanic activities during the Pleistocene Epoch. Following the 
cessation of volcanic activity of the Koolau Volcano, alluvial soil materials were deposited 
in a deltaic or estuarine environment in the area of Pearl Harbor. 
The sea level fluctuated several times, receding perhaps 300 feet below and rising 
about 100 feet above the present sea level. Concurrent with a rise in sea level during the 
late Pleistocene era, eruption of the Salt Lake‐Makalapa member of the Honolulu 
Volcanic Series mantled the older alluvium with thick deposits of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated to form volcanic tuff (locally known as “mudrock”) layers. Subsequent 
erosion dissected and thinned those mantles of volcanic tuff. 
During the recent epoch, as sea level rose to its current stand, the area was 
covered with soft and loose lagoonal marine deposits. These deposits were subsequently 
covered with man‐made fills placed during the middle portion of this century for land 
reclamation projects during construction of the existing Pearl Harbor Naval Shipyard. The 
waterfront facility project site is located at the edge of the former Pearl Harbor shoreline. 
The subsurface conditions of the project site generally consist of fill materials overlying 
coralline deposits and highly weathered volcanic tuff materials. 
2.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed five main stratigraphic units across the project area within 
the waterfront facility. The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock materials of varying 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 6 
Hawaii • California 
character and engineering properties. The five main stratigraphic units are listed below in 
the general order of geologic age, beginning with the younger unit and ending with the 
older unit: 
1. 
Recent Fill (Rf) 
2. 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
3. 
Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
4. 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
5. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
It should be noted that the surface fills are generally underlain by interbedded 
layers of recent alluvium, volcanic tuffs, coralline detritus, coral/sandstone formations, 
and older alluvium.  The following subsections briefly describe the general characteristics 
of each of the above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, experience with 
similar materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at 
selected locations across the project site. 
Recent Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low-lying areas that were filled during 
urbanization. The majority of these low-lying areas are underlain at relatively 
shallow depths by recent alluvium and were originally marsh areas. Small parts of 
the filled lands are underlain by older alluvial soil deposits. In areas underlain by 
the recent alluvium, the fill materials generally consist of clayey silts and sandy 
silts with stiff to hard consistencies. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to 
make any general statement regarding their engineering properties or construction 
in these areas. 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils generally are the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly underwater and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated [OCR (Over 
Consolidation Ratio) < 1.0] sediments, which settle under the weight of minimal 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii • California 
overburden or structural loading. Recent alluvium generally is found within some 
stream channels and bordering some stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of 
streams, and within broad valley floors and basin features surrounded by hills. 
These soils may also be found in proximity to the swamp and tidal flat 
environments. The following subunits are used in this report, where applicable. 
Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel-sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with 
fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized by 
a moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile mollusk 
shells. 
Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Tuff from the vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series consists of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated or hardened to form a medium hard rock that resembles siltstone or 
sandstone, locally called “mudrock”. The tuff is frequently found overlying coralline 
deposits or older alluvium units. This formation has relatively low permeability, and 
groundwater generally is not encountered in this formation. When it does occur, it 
is usually in the form of seepage along joints. 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii • California 
Coral Limestone (Qls) 
This unit consists of fossil remnants of coral-algal reefs and their related detrital 
debris deposits. In Hawaii, the detrital deposits tend to be the dominant portion of 
any coralline rock assemblage. The detrital deposits are weakly to strongly 
cemented coralline debris consisting of gravel, sand, and silt-sized clastic 
fragments. Some of the coralline fragments were dissolved, and the solution 
cavities were filled with cemented and/or uncemented fine sands and silts. The 
following subunits are used in this report, where applicable. 
Coralline Detritus 
This subunit consists of granular detritus broken from reef formations. The 
soils generally consist of medium dense to dense sandy gravel and gravelly 
sands. Silts and clays are only present in minor or trace amounts. Detritus 
may also be weakly cemented with occasional moderate to strong 
cementation. 
Coral Formation 
This subunit is composed of coral and calcareous algae in their original 
growth positions or detrital deposits, which have become moderately to well 
cemented. 
Sandstone 
Sandstone is a sedimentary rock composed mainly of cemented sand-sized 
particles (clasts) consisting of individual mineral particles or grains of the 
weathered parent rock. The sedimentary particles comprising sandstone 
typically are derived from the breakdown of parent material into sedimentary 
particles. The sedimentary particles typically are eroded, transported and 
sorted by water or air, and deposited as layers of sedimentary materials. 
Over time, the sediments may be buried by other sediments or geologic 
layers, and the burial pressure combined with a cementing agent act to 
consolidate the grains and cement the pore space between the grains to 
form hardened sandstone. Typical cementing agents may be calcium 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii • California 
carbonate (common in calcareous sand deposits of Hawaii) or silica and 
some clays.  
Most sandstones in Hawaii are light brown in color and calcareous being 
derived from the breakdown of parent coralline and reef materials into 
sand-sized particles. The cementing agent in most calcareous sandstone is 
calcium carbonate. Inclusions of basaltic rock fragments, marine shells, and 
other fragments of broken coral may be cemented in the sandstone layers. 
Sandstones containing such clast inclusions commonly are referred to as 
beachrock in Hawaii, having been derived from available shoreline geologic 
materials.  
The strength (hardness) of sandstone generally depends on the grain size, 
degree of sorting, and degree of cementation of the sedimentary materials. 
The weathering and decomposition of calcareous sandstone may be 
accelerated by exposure to acidic water, which attacks the calcareous 
cement binding the sand grains, and causes a slow exfoliation of the 
outermost exposed layers of the sandstone outcropping. Sandstone may or 
may not contain visible bedding surfaces. Aeolian (wind derived) sandstone 
may exhibit cross-bedding structures that developed during the initial 
deposition of the sand layers. 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time 
by more recent deposits to become consolidated, and then subsequently 
uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit 
generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which 
have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit may also contain artesian 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii • California 
groundwater from the basal freshwater system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
may resemble saprolite soil deposits, but they are clearly differentiable by the 
layered characteristics. Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high 
shrink/swell potential when subjected to fluctuations in moisture. The following 
subunits are used in this report, where applicable. 
Older Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very stiff to hard.  
Old Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in stiff to very stiff consistency and/or medium dense to dense 
relative density with various degrees of cementation. In some cases, 
strongly cemented deposits form siltstones. Gravel sized fragments are 
rare. Frequently, the silts are organic in origin and may have a mild to strong 
organic odor.  
2.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
The subsurface conditions at the inboard alignment of Wharf B21 were explored 
by drilling and sampling three borings, designated as Boring Nos. 201 through 203, 
extending to the maximum depth drilled of about 102 feet below the existing ground 
surface. In addition, the subsurface conditions at Wharf B20 and the outbound area of 
Wharf B21 were previously explored by Geolabs, Inc. in 2002 and 2020 by drilling and 
sampling six borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 6, and four borings, 
designated as 101 through 104, respectively, extending to the maximum depths drilled 
about 75.5 to 159 feet below the existing ground surface. The approximate boring 
locations are shown on the Overall Boring Location Plan, Plate 2. 
In general, the mudline encountered at the outboard edge of the wharves (B20 and 
B21) is anticipated at about Elevations +67 to +64 feet United States Navy 
Datum (USND). The approximate boring locations near the outboard edge of Wharves 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10

groundwater levels in the drilled on-land borings at depths of between about 8.2 and 
13.1 feet below the existing ground surface. The groundwater levels measured generally 
correspond to about Elevations +102.8 and +96.9 feet USND. It should be noted that 
groundwater levels are subject to change due to tidal fluctuations, storm surge conditions, 
rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, and other factors.

2.4 
Seismic Design Considerations 
We understand that the project seismic design is in accordance with ASCE 61-14, 
Seismic Design of Piers and Wharves, 2014 Edition and ASCE 7-10, Minimum Design 
Loads for Buildings and Other Structures, 2010 Edition per UFC 4-152-01 (2017). Based 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii • California 
on ASCE 7-10, the project site may be subject to seismic activity, and seismic design 
considerations will need to be addressed. The following subsections provide discussions 
on the soil profile type for seismic design and liquefaction design consideration at the 
project site. 
2.4.1 Earthquakes and Seismicity 
In general, earthquakes that occur throughout the world are caused by shifts in the 
tectonic plates. In contrast, earthquake activity in Hawaii is linked primarily to 
volcanic activity. Therefore, earthquake activity in Hawaii generally occurs before 
or during volcanic eruptions. In addition, earthquakes may result from the 
underground movement of magma that comes close to the surface but does not 
erupt. The Island of Hawaii experiences thousands of earthquakes each year, but 
most are so small that they can only be detected by sensitive instruments. 
However, some of the earthquakes are strong enough to be felt, and a few cause 
minor to moderate damage. 
In general, earthquakes associated with volcanic activity are most common on the 
Island of Hawaii. Earthquakes that are directly associated with the movement of 
magma are concentrated beneath the active Kilauea and Mauna Loa Volcanoes 
on the Island of Hawaii. Because the majority of earthquakes in Hawaii (over 
90 percent) are related to volcanic activity, the risk of seismic activity and degree 
of ground shaking diminishes with increased distance from the Island of Hawaii. 
The Island of Hawaii has experienced numerous earthquakes greater than 
Magnitude 5 (M5+); however, earthquakes are not confined only to the Island of 
Hawaii. 
To a lesser degree, the Island of Maui has experienced several earthquakes greater 
than Magnitude 5. Therefore, moderate to strong earthquakes have occurred in the 
County of Maui. The effects of earthquakes occurring on the Islands of Hawaii and 
Maui may be felt on the Island of Oahu. For example, small landslides occurred on 
the Island of Oahu as a result of the Maui Earthquake of 1938 (M6.8). Some houses 
on the Island of Oahu were reportedly damaged as a result of the Lanai Earthquake 
of 1871 (M7+). 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii • California 
Due to the relatively short period of documented earthquake monitoring in the State 
of Hawaii, information pertaining to earthquakes that were felt on the Island of Oahu 
may not be complete. In general, over the last 150 years of recorded history, we are 
not aware of reported earthquakes greater than Magnitude 6 occurring on the Island 
of Oahu. Based on available information, we understand that an earthquake of about 
Magnitude 5.6 occurred on June 28, 1948 in the vicinity of the Island of Oahu, 
possibly along the hypothesized and controversial Diamond Head Fault feature. 
The Diamond Head Fault feature is believed to extend northeasterly away from the 
southeastern tip of the Island of Oahu. The Diamond Head Fault feature may be 
related to the widely documented Molokai Fracture Zone located on the sea floor in 
the vicinity of the Hawaiian Islands. Despite only the moderate tremor intensity, the 
resulting damage was reportedly widespread and included broken windows, ruptured 
masonry building walls, and a broken underground water main. In addition, some 
areas on the Island of Oahu, including the Tantalus, Iwilei, and Tripler areas, reported 
more intense ground shaking, severe enough to have cracked reinforced concrete. 
2.4.2 Liquefaction Potential 
Soil liquefaction is a condition where saturated cohesionless soils located near the 
ground surface undergo a substantial loss of strength due to the build-up of excess 
pore pressures resulting from cyclic stress applications induced by earthquakes. 
In this process, when the loose saturated sand deposit is subjected to vibration 
(such as during an earthquake), the soil tends to densify and decrease in volume 
causing an increase in pore pressure. If drainage is unable to occur rapidly enough 
to dissipate the build-up of pore water pressure, the effective stress (internal 
strength) of the soil is reduced. 
Under sustained vibrations, the pore water pressure build-up could equal the 
overburden pressure, essentially reducing the soil shear strength to zero and 
causing it to behave as a viscous fluid. During liquefaction, the soil acquires a 
mobility sufficient to permit both horizontal and vertical movements, and if not 
confined, will result in significant deformations. Soils most susceptible to 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii • California 
liquefaction are loose, uniformly graded, fine-grained sands and loose silts with 
little cohesion. 
Our field exploration encountered loose/soft harbor/lagoonal deposits in several 
borings drilled for the project. Based on the subsurface conditions encountered, 
the harbor/lagoonal deposits in some areas consist of very soft clays/silts and 
loose sands/gravels. We anticipate that the loose harbor/lagoonal deposits 
encountered near the inboard alignment of Wharf B21 may be considered as 
potentially liquefiable soils. 
2.4.3 Soil Profile Type for Seismic Design 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed in an effort to closely analyze 
the seismic design considerations at the site. We performed three seismic shear 
wave velocity profiling tests using seismic cone penetration testing (SCPT) 
equipment extending to a depth of approximately 100 feet below the existing 
ground surface. Results of the seismic shear wave velocity testing are presented in 
Appendix B. Results of previous seismic shear wave velocity profiling test conducted 
by Geolabs, Inc. in 2020 is included in Appendix C. 
To analyze the subsurface soil parameters for seismic design considerations, 
shear wave velocity profiling was performed at discrete intervals extending to 
approximately 99.9 to 100.4 feet below the ground surface. Based on the shear 
wave velocity profiling, the weighted average shear wave velocity for the 
subsurface materials within the upper 100 feet of the profile is on the order of about 
1,028 to 1,123 feet per second at the test location. 
Because the project site is underlain by more than 10 feet of very loose soils, the 
project site should be classified from a seismic analysis standpoint as being Site 
Class F based on Chapter 20 of ASCE 7-10. Per Section 20.3.1 of ASCE 7-10, a 
site-specific ground motion study will need to be performed to determine the 
appropriate values for seismic design considerations. It should be noted that the 
project site would qualify as Site Class D based on the results of the seismic shear 
wave velocity testing only.

Based on the site-specific seismic hazard analysis performed by EMI, we understand 
that the design peak ground acceleration (PGA) is determined as 0.25g under the 
code-based design (DE) level. 
Based on our analysis and the anticipated subsurface conditions, we believe the 
phenomenon of soil liquefaction may occur near the northern end of Wharf B21. 
Based on our analyses, it appears that the loose lagoonal deposits near that area 
have a factor of safety of 0.3 to 1.0 against liquefaction during a seismic event of 
Magnitude 6 with a design peak ground acceleration of up to 0.250g. Therefore, the 
project site could be subjected to appreciable ground settlements of up 23 to 
27 inches in the event of liquefaction during an earthquake with a Magnitude 6.

SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Based on our field exploration, the outboard edge of the Wharves B20 and B21 
encountered the mudline at about Elevations +67 to +64 feet USND. The outboard edge 
of Wharf B20 and B21 consisted of loose/soft harbor sediment and volcanic tuff overlying 
stiff alluvium and dense coral formation. In general, the inboard alignment of Wharf B20, 
behind the existing bulkhead, consisted of weathered upper volcanic tuff and coralline 
deposits overlying stiff clays and weathered lower volcanic tuff. Along the inboard 
alignment of Wharf B21, the subsurface conditions consisted of loose/soft lagoonal 
deposits about 40 to 60 feet thick overlying dense coralline material and interbedded 
layers of stiff alluvium and weathered volcanic tuff. 
We encountered groundwater levels in the drilled on-land borings at depths of 
about 8.2 and 13.1 feet below the existing pavement surface. The groundwater levels 
measured generally correspond to about Elevations +102.8 and +96.9 feet USND. It 
should be noted that groundwater levels are subject to change due to tidal fluctuations, 
storm surge conditions, rainfall, time of year, seasonal precipitation, surface water runoff, 
and other factors. 
Based on our field exploration and the structural load demands provided, we 
believe that the new wharves may be supported on a deep foundation system consisting 
of 24-inch octagonal precast, prestressed concrete piles along the outboard edge. The 
piles at Wharf B20 would derive vertical support primarily from end-bearing on the 
volcanic tuff formation (mudrock). However, based on the anticipated subsurface 
conditions at Wharf B21, the 24-inch concrete piles will most likely need to extend to 
deeper depths to derive support from the dense coralline material. Predrilling will be 
required in order to install the concrete piles to the designed tip elevation and to avoid 
encountering obstructions anticipated during pile driving operations. 
In order to achieve the required vertical compressive and lateral resistance 
capacities, we recommend 36-inch cast-in-place drilled shafts to support the new wharves 
inboard alignment. The drilled shafts would derive vertical support primarily from adhesion 
between the shaft and surrounding soils. Temporary casing may be required to reduce 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii • California 
the potential for caving-in of the boreholes due to the presence of the loose 
coralline/lagoonal deposits and shallow groundwater levels encountered in the borings. 
In addition, placement of concrete by tremie methods will be required for the drilled shafts 
because groundwater will be encountered within the depths of the drilled shafts. 
To mitigate the potentially liquefiable area in the northern portion of Wharf B21, we 
recommend installing overlapping jet-grouted columns arranged in a grid pattern. The 
jet-grouted column grid pattern would confine the liquefiable soils to mitigate the effects 
of liquefaction. Due to the variation of the soft/loose soil thickness, we recommend 
performing a probing program prior to the production jet grout column installation, to 
define the final production jet grout column length. In addition, a test jet grouting program 
is also recommended to examine the design parameters of the jet-grouted column and 
the effects of the jet grouting mitigation. 
Detailed discussions and recommendations for these items and other geotechnical 
aspects of the project are presented in the following sections. 
3.1 
Driven Pile Foundations 
Based on the results of the field exploration, we anticipate subsurface conditions 
along the outboard alignment will encounter the mudline at about +67 to +64 feet USND. 
The anticipated soil profile for Wharf B20 consists of soft/loose harbor sediments 
overlying interbedded layers of volcanic tuff formation (mudrock), stiff alluvium, and dense 
coralline materials. For Wharf B21, we anticipate a relatively thick layer of loose/soft 
lagoonal deposit overlying stiff alluvium with interbedded layers of weathered volcanic tuff 
underlain by dense coralline material. 
Based on the design concept and our engineering analyses, we envision that 
24-inch octagonal precast, prestressed concrete piles may be used to support the 
proposed new wharves with the planned crane rails along the outboard alignment. The 
24-inch concrete piles at Wharf B20 would derive vertical support primarily from 
end-bearing on the volcanic tuff formation (mudrock). However, based on the anticipated 
subsurface conditions at Wharf B21, the 24-inch concrete piles will most likely need to 
extend to deeper depths to derive support from the dense coralline material.

3.1.1 Predrilling 
Based on our experience at the vicinity of the site, obstructions encountered within 
the harbor sediments are common. Therefore, we believe predrilling generally would 
be required for the installation of the pile foundations and should be included in the 
contract documents.  
In addition, for Wharf B20, we recommend socketing the driven piles into the mudrock 
layer. Therefore, the tip of the predrilling should be located a minimum of about 4 feet 
below the top of the mudrock. For Wharf B21, we recommend predrilling through the 
relatively thin layer of mudrock and socketing the driven piles into the underlying 
alluvium material.  
Geolabs should verify and/or modify the required predrilling depths during the test 
pile program depending on the subsurface conditions encountered and pile driving 
equipment used. We wish to emphasize that the diameter of the predrilled holes 
should have a diameter only as large as the diameter of the piles to provide the driven 
piles with sufficient soil contact for lateral load resistance. If an over-sized predrilled 
hole is encountered during construction, the annular space between the piles and 
predrilled holes should be backfilled with pea gravel or grouted with tremie concrete. 
3.1.2 Lateral Load Resistance 
We anticipate that the driven pile foundation will not be designed to resist the lateral 
mooring loads due to the long unsupported length of the pile. We also anticipate 
that the precast, prestressed concrete piles will need to be furnished in one piece 
for pile installation.

To support the structural modeling analyses, the tabular and graphical data of the 
“p-y” curves for the concrete driven piles are presented on Plates 5.1 through 5.5. 
We recommend using the lateral spring of the concrete driven piles with caution to 
consider the strain compatibility to the in-board drilled shafts, which have a much 
greater lateral spring constant. 
3.1.3 Pile Foundation Settlements 
Settlements of the driven pile foundations will primarily result from elastic 
compression of the pile member and subgrade response. We estimate the total 
settlement of the pile-supported foundations to be less than 0.75 inches with 
differential settlements between columns supported on piles not exceeding about 
one-half of the estimated total settlement. We believe that these settlements are 
essentially elastic and should occur as the loads are applied. 
3.1.4 Pile Hammer Selection 
The piles should be driven with a hammer capable of delivering a rated energy of 
up to 75,000 foot-pounds (104,138 joules) per blow. The hammer should be 
equipped with an energy control level such that driving within the loose/soft soils 
may be controlled at a lower energy to reduce tensile stress development within 
the concrete piles, which could damage or cause cracking of the piles. 
Determination of driving equipment to be used for this project should take into 
consideration the “matching” of the pile hammer with the weight of the piles to be 
driven. This will result in the pile hammer efficiently delivering the required energy 
to the top of the pile and also reducing the potential for damage to the piles. 
Geolabs should review the pile driving equipment proposed to evaluate whether 
the equipment is capable of installing the piles to the recommended capacity. 
The contractor should forward pile and driving equipment data forms for review 
and evaluation prior to construction. Geolabs should review the submittal and 
assess the drivability of piles with the use of the selected hammer. A wave equation 
analysis should be performed after the selection of the pile driving equipment to 
assess the driveability of the piles and pile driving criteria. Due to possible variation 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii • California 
in the length of piles, unit prices should be obtained during bidding for add-ons, 
shorter piles, etc.

should be confirmed after the selection of the pile driving equipment and during test 
pile driving to accommodate variations in the subsurface conditions in the field.

In general, each pile should be driven continuously without interruption. A pile may 
be rejected or its design capacity may be reduced when the driving resistance is 
interrupted for more than 4 hours and the pile cannot be driven to the required depth 
and/or meet the pile driving criteria. During soft driving (i.e., when the pile tip is within 
soft and/or loose soils), the hammer stroke (or energy) should be carefully controlled 
such that significant tensile stresses do not develop within the pile. 
Because variations in soil strength characteristics are likely to exist in the bearing 
soils, the supporting capacity of each pile must be confirmed in the field by our 
representative during pile driving. It should be noted that the pile design was 
developed from our pile analysis using the field exploration data. Therefore, a test 
pile program should be carried out to confirm our preliminary pile design and to 
establish pile driving criteria. It should be noted that the production pile driving criteria 
may be revised based on the results of the test pile program.

results. When the final foundation plans are available, Geolabs should be consulted 
for selection of the test pile locations. 
Test piles should be cast at least 10 feet longer than the estimated pile length to 
account for potential variation in the subsurface conditions. Each test pile should be 
instrumented for monitoring by a pile driving analyzer (PDA) to observe the stresses 
developed in the test piles and to evaluate the pile bearing capacity.  Instrumentation 
and monitoring of a pile by a pile driving analyzer is also known as “dynamic load 
testing.” In addition, the test pile program should provide flexibility to allow for addition 
or relocation of the test piles, as may be deemed necessary by our office, to suit the 
conditions encountered during the test pile driving.  Re-striking of the test piles a 
minimum of 72 hours after the initial pile driving will be required as part of the test pile 
program. 
The test piles should be driven with the same hammer that will be used for 
production pile driving. The test pile program should be conducted under 
continuous observation of a representative from Geolabs to assist in determining 
when the test piles may be stopped and to provide guidance should additional 
measures be required due to highly various driving conditions. Geolabs should 
evaluate the load carrying capacity of the test piles and production pile embedment 
lengths based on the results of the test pile driving and load testing. 
Therefore, a Geolabs representative should be present during the pile driving 
operations to observe the actual driving behavior and to further evaluate the field 
performance. Our observation of the pile driving operations is necessary to confirm

Drilled Shaft Foundations 
Based on the provided information, we believe a deep foundation system consisting 
of cast-in-place concrete drilled shafts should be used to support the proposed new wharves 
along the inboard alignment. The drilled shafts would derive vertical support primarily from 
adhesion between the shaft and the surrounding soil. 
Based on the subsurface soil conditions encountered at the site, we have 
performed preliminary engineering analyses to estimate the shaft lengths required to 
support the proposed structure. Our engineering analyses are based on the following 
assumptions: 
1. 
The subsurface conditions encountered in the borings at the site. 
2. 
Top of the drilled shaft is set at about +107 feet USND. 
3. 
Axial loading of up to 600 kips for dead-plus-live loads. 
4. 
Mooring lateral loading of up to 108 kips. 
5. 
A level mudline at all drilled shaft locations with the exception of the drilled 
shafts between grid lines 22 and 35, where there is a mudline slope of 
approximately 1.9H:1V. 
It should be noted that the drilled shaft design along the inboard side of Wharf B21 is 
divided into two areas, Zone-1 and Zone-2. The subsurface profile in Zone-1 consists of the 
stiff/dense soil conditions in the southern portion of Wharf B21, whereas Zone-2 comprises 
of soft/loose soils over stiff alluvium with interbedded tuff formation in the northern portion of 
Wharf B21. We recommend additional probing along the inboard of Wharves B20 and B21 
to better distinguish where the subsurface begins to diverge.

3.2.1 Uplift Load Resistance 
In general, uplift loads imposed on the foundations may be resisted by a 
combination of the dead weight of the drilled shaft and by shear along the shaft 
surface and the adjacent soils. Considering that the drilled shafts are designed 
based on adhesion between the shaft and the surrounding soils, we recommend 
an allowable uplift load capacity on the order of up to 500 kips to resist uplift loads. 
The recommended uplift load capacity includes the weight of the drilled shaft. The 
structural capacity of the shaft member in tension should be checked by the project 
structural engineer. 
3.2.2 Lateral Load Resistance 
Lateral loads imposed on the structure may be resisted by a combination of the 
lateral load capacity of the drilled shafts and the passive pressure acting against 
the near-vertical faces of the footings/grade beams. Passive earth pressure 
against the near-vertical faces of the footings/grade beam may be estimated using 
equivalent fluid pressures of 300 and 150 pounds per square foot per foot of depth 
(pcf) for above and below groundwater conditions, respectively. 
The lateral load resistance of drilled shafts is a function of the stiffness of the 
surrounding soil, the stiffness of the drilled shaft, allowable deflection at the top of the 
drilled shaft, and the induced moment in the drilled shaft. The lateral load analyses 
were performed using the program LPILE-plus for Windows, which is a 
microcomputer adaptation of a finite difference, laterally loaded deep foundation 
program originally developed at the University of Texas at Austin. The program 
solves for deflection and bending moment along the deep foundation under lateral 
loads as a function of depth. 
The analyses were carried out with the use of non-linear “p-y” curves to represent 
anticipated soil moduli at various depths.  The analyses were performed for laterally 
loaded 36-inch diameter drilled shafts. Our analyses were based on the compressive 
loads recommended for the shaft and a fixed-head boundary condition at the top of 
the shaft. The maximum induced moments for the fixed-head boundary condition 
should occur near the top of the shaft.

the non-linear nature of the soils.

3.2.3 Foundation Settlements 
Settlement of the drilled shaft foundation will occur as a result of compression of 
the drilled shaft and subgrade response of the foundation embedded in the 
subsurface soil conditions described in this report. Total drilled shaft settlements 
are estimated to be on the order of less than 1 inch. Differential settlements 
between the drilled shafts are estimated to be less than 0.5 inches. We believe 
that a significant portion of the settlement is elastic and should occur as the loads 
are applied. Post-construction foundation settlements should be approximately 
0.5 inches or less. 
3.2.4 Drilled Shaft Construction Considerations 
In general, the performance of drilled shafts depends significantly upon the 
contractor’s method of installation and construction procedures. The following 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 27 
Hawaii • California 
conditions would have a significant effect on the effectiveness and cost of the 
drilled shaft foundations. 
The weathered volcanic tuff formation was encountered at shallow depths during 
our field exploration. Therefore, coring into the weathered volcanic tuff formations 
encountered in the borings will be required. The drilled shaft contractor will need 
to demonstrate that the proposed drilling equipment (and coring tools) will be able 
to install the drilled shafts to the recommended depths and dimensions by the 
performance of a trial shaft program. 
As mentioned above, relatively thick soft/loose soil conditions may present in the 
northern portion of Wharf B21, which may require temporary casing during the 
drilled shaft installation to prevent the cave-in condition. Care should be exercised 
during the removal of the temporary casing to reduce the potential for "necking" of 
the drilled shaft.  Therefore, a minimum 5-foot head of concrete above the bottom 
of the casing or adequate concrete head to counter hydrostatic pressures should 
be maintained during the removal of the casing.  The anticipated shallow 
groundwater level at the project site may also pose construction difficulties since 
proper observation of the sides and bottoms of the drilled shaft may not be 
possible. 
The sidewalls of the drilled shaft holes will need to be “rifled” with grooves to 
provide the necessary contact and side shaft resistance assumed in our analyses.  
The grooves should be at least 2 inches deep by 3 inches wide and should run 
spirally along the shaft circumference at a pitch of about 12 inches. The “rifling” 
procedure may be omitted in the hard volcanic tuff formation. The “rifling” 
procedure will be required in the weathered volcanic tuff and coral formations. 
Drilling by methods utilizing drilling fluids is generally not recommended unless 
specifically approved by Geolabs upon evaluation of the type of drilling fluids 
proposed. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 28 
Hawaii • California 
3.2.5 Workmanship 
The load carrying capacities of the drilled shafts depend, to a large extent, on the 
contact between the drilled shafts and the surrounding materials. Therefore, proper 
construction techniques are important. The contractor should exercise care in 
drilling the shaft holes, rifling the sides of the drilled shaft holes, and placing 
concrete into the holes. 
Due to the shallow groundwater table anticipated within the depths of the drilled 
shaft excavations, concrete placement by tremie methods will be required during 
construction of the drilled shafts under such conditions. The concrete should be 
placed in a suitable manner by displacing the water in an upward fashion from the 
bottom of the drilled hole. A low-shrink concrete mix with high slump (7 to 9-inch 
slump range) should be used to provide close contact between the drilled shafts 
and the surrounding soils. The concrete should be placed in a suitable manner to 
reduce the potential for segregation of the aggregates from the concrete mix. In 
addition, the concrete should be placed promptly after drilling (within 24 hours) to 
reduce the potential for softening of the sides of the drilled holes. 
It is imperative that a representative from Geolabs be present at the site to observe 
the drilling and installation of drilled shafts during construction. Although the drilled 
shafts are designed based primarily on skin friction, the bottom of the drilled hole 
should be relatively free of loose materials prior to the placement of concrete. 
Therefore, observation of the drilled shaft installation operations by Geolabs is 
necessary to confirm the assumed subsurface conditions and should be 
designated as a “Special Inspection” item. 
In consideration of the difficulties of the subsurface conditions at the site and the 
complexity of the drilled shaft foundation for this project, we recommend that the 
drilled shaft contractor for this project be pre-qualified during the bidding process. 
3.2.6 Trial Shaft Program 
A trial shaft program is highly recommended for this project considering the high 
structural loads demanded and variation of the subsurface conditions.  Considering 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 29 
Hawaii • California 
the diameter and structural load capacities of the drilled shafts, we recommend that 
a trial shaft program be undertaken to fulfill the following objectives: 
To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed by the 
contractor to install the drilled shafts to the required depths. 
To confirm or modify the sizes (diameter and length) of the drilled shafts. 
To assess the contractor’s method of placing and extracting the temporary 
casing for the drilled shaft. 
To assess the contractor’s method of tremie concrete placement. 
To achieve these objectives, we recommend that the trial shaft program consist of 
drilling one 36-inch diameter trial shaft extending to a depth of 65 feet below the 
design cut-off elevation. The location of the trial shaft should be near, but outside 
of the inboard alignment within Wharf B21 Zone-1 area. After drilling the trial shaft, 
the trial shaft may be converted into the load test shaft if it is deemed acceptable 
by Geolabs. 
3.2.7 Bi-Directional Load Test 
A test shaft program is highly recommended for this project considering the high 
structural loads demanded and variation of the subsurface conditions.  Considering 
the diameter and structural load capacities of the drilled shafts, we recommend 
conducting bi-directional axial load tests for the project. The load tests should be 
conducted on sacrificial 3-foot diameter drilled shafts (two load tests total) extending 
to depths of about 65 feet (Wharf B21 Zone-1) and 125 feet (Wharf B21 Zone-1) 
below the ground surface, respectively, for performance of two instrumented load 
tests. 
The results of the load tests will be used to confirm or modify the estimated tip 
elevations of the production drilled shafts. In general, we recommend that the load 
test shaft be structurally reinforced and instrumented with vibrating wire embedment 
strain gauges for load testing purposes. As a minimum, two embedment strain 
gauges should be place at each level, starting from the bottom at an elevation of 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 30 
Hawaii • California 
about 5 feet above and below the load cells and subsequently at about 10-foot 
intervals. 
Due to the relatively high capacities recommended for the drilled shafts, a 
conventional load test would not be practical and would be costly to conduct. 
Therefore, we recommend that bi-directional axial load tests be conducted using 
an expandable load cell (Osterberg Load Cell). The bi-directional load test 
separately tests the shear resistance and end-bearing components of the drilled 
shaft by loading the shaft in two directions (upward for shear resistance, and 
downward for end-bearing and shear resistance).  
The Osterberg Load Cells should be capable of applying a load of at least 
1,200 kips in each direction. The expandable base load cell will need to be 
attached to the reinforcing cage of the load test shaft prior to lowing the cage in 
place. 
The drilled shaft load test should be performed in general accordance with the 
Quick Load Test Method of ASTM D1143. The load test shaft should be loaded to 
failure to evaluate the ultimate side shear resistance of the shaft. Installation of the 
expandable load cells, installation of the embedment strain gauges, performance 
of the bi-directional axial load tests, and presentation of the load test data should 
be performed by a professional experienced in these types of load testing 
procedures. The load test shafts should be loaded at increments of about 50 to 
100 kips and should be held for a minimum of 4 hours at the 600-kips, 800-kip, 
1,000-kip and 1,200-kip load increments to evaluate the potential for creep effects. 
We recommend that a representative from Geolabs observe the installation and 
performance of the instrumented load test on the drilled shaft. It should be noted 
that the drilled shaft design was developed from our analysis using the field 
exploration data. Therefore, observation of the drilled shaft installation operations 
by Geolabs is a vital part of the foundation design to confirm the design 
assumptions.

W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 31 
Hawaii • California 
3.2.8 Non-Destructive Integrity Testing 
Based on the critical nature of the drilled shaft foundations, we recommend 
conducting non-destructive integrity testing on the production drilled shafts for the 
project. Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL), has been gaining widespread use and 
acceptance for integrity testing of drilled shafts. 
Crosshole Sonic Logging techniques are based on the propagation of sound 
waves through concrete. In general, the actual velocity of sound wave 
propagation in concrete is dependent on the concrete material properties, 
geometry of the element and the wave length of the sound waves. When 
ultrasonic frequencies are generated, Pressure (P) waves and Shear (S) waves 
travel through the concrete. If anomalies are contained in the concrete, the 
anomalies will reduce the P-wave travel velocity in the concrete. Anomalies in the 
drilled shaft concrete may include soil particles, gravel, water, voids, 
contaminated concrete, and highly segregated constituent particles. 
The transit time of an ultrasonic P-wave signal may be measured between an 
ultrasonic transmitter and receiver in two parallel water filled access tubes placed 
into the concrete during construction. The P-wave velocity can be obtained by 
dividing the measured transit time from the distance between the transmitter and 
receiver. Therefore, anomalies may be detected (if they exist). 
To reduce the potential de-bonding between the access tubes and the 
surrounding concrete, we recommend that the access tubes consist of standard 
steel pipe with a minimum inside diameter of 2 inches. In addition, the access 
tube should be equipped with watertight coupling. In general, the access tubes 
should be securely attached to the interior of the reinforcing cage as near to 
parallel as possible in the drilled shaft. We recommend casting a minimum of 
three access tubes into the concrete of the 36-inch diameter drilled shafts. 
In addition, the access tubes should extend from the bottom of the drilled shaft 
reinforcing cage to at least 3.5 feet above the top of the shaft. The bottom of the 
access tube should be permanently capped. It is imperative that joints required to 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 32 
Hawaii • California 
achieve the full length of the access tubs be watertight. It is the responsibility of 
the contractor to take extra care to prevent damaging the access tubes during the 
placement of the reinforcing cage into the drilled hole. The tubes should be filled 
with potable water as soon as possible, but not later than 4 hours after the 
concrete placement. Subsequently, the top of the access tubes should be capped 
with watertight caps. 
The Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL) test of drilled shafts should be conducted 
after a least 7 days of curing time, but no later than 28 days after concrete 
placement. In addition, the CSL test of drilled shafts should be performed in 
general accordance with ASTM D6760. In the event that a drilled shaft is found to 
have significant anomalies and/or is suspected to be defective based on the CSL 
testing and/or field observations, the drilled shaft should be cored to evaluate the 
integrity of the concrete in the drilled shaft. After completion of the crosshole sonic 
logging of the drilled shafts, all the access tubes should be filled with grout of the 
same strength as the drilled shaft concrete.

The lateral load analyses were performed using the program GROUP for Windows, 
which is a microcomputer adaptation of a finite difference, laterally loaded group of piles 
program developed by ENSOFT, Inc. The program solves for deflection and bending 
moment along a pile under lateral loads as a function of depth. The analysis was carried out 
with the use of non-linear “p-y” curves to represent soil moduli. The lateral deflection was 
then computed using the appropriate soil moduli at various depths. 
The analyses were performed for a group of six laterally loaded 36-inch diameter 
drilled shafts. Our analyses were based on the fixed-head boundary condition at the top 
of each drilled shaft. The maximum induced moments for the fixed-head boundary 
condition generally occur near the top of the shaft. The lateral group foundation stiffness, 
expressed in kips per inch of deflection was estimated for each loading scenarios. A group 
reduction factor of 0.85 and 0.65 were included to the pile group. It should be noted that 
lateral foundation stiffness should only be used for deflections of up to about 1 inch due 
to the non-linear nature of the soils. The tabular and graphical data of the “p-y” curves are 
presented on Plates 7.1 through 7.13.

summarized in the table below.

3.4 
Fender Pile Foundations 
We envision new 24-inch square precast, prestressed concrete piles will be 
installed to support the wharf fender system at the project site location. We understand 
that fender pile groups will consist of five 24-inch square concrete piles. 
Based on the anticipated subsurface conditions, we believe the new concrete 
fender piles should be installed through the harbor sediment deposits and/or lagoonal 
deposits to provide adequate lateral resistance. The lateral load resistance of the fender 
pile is a function of the stiffness of the surrounding soil, the stiffness of the pile, allowable 
deflection at the top of the pile, and the induced moment in the pile. The lateral load 
analyses were performed using the program LPILE-plus for Windows, which is a 
microcomputer adaption of a finite difference, laterally loaded deep foundation program 
originally developed at the University of Texas at Austin. The program solves for 
deflection and bending moment along the deep foundation under lateral loads as a 
function of depth. 
The analyses were carried out with the use of non-linear “p-y” curves to represent 
anticipated soil moduli at various depths. The analyses were performed for laterally 
loaded 24-inch square concrete piles based on the following structural loading cases.

3.5 
Jet Grouting 
Based on our analyses, it appears that the loose lagoonal deposits near the 
northern portion of Wharf B21 could be subjected to appreciable ground settlements of 
up 23 to 27 inches in the event of liquefaction during an earthquake with a Magnitude 6. 
The effects of the liquefaction would also likely generate much higher lateral earth 
pressure on the sheetpile bulkhead, induce potential distress on the existing metal 
sheetpiles. In order to mitigate the liquefaction potential, we recommend installing 
jet-grouted columns in the area shown on the Jet Grout Cell Layout, Plate 10. 
In general, jet grouting is a ground improvement technique used to mitigate 
liquefiable soils during earthquakes. This method utilizes a special drill bit and injection 
monitor with radial horizontal nozzles to produce stabilized soil-cement columns. The 
jet-grouting technique is a process that produces soil-cement columns by injecting streams 
of grout slurry through horizontal jets at high pressures to erode, mix, replace, and cement 
soils. The horizontal jets of cement grout slurry cuts and mixes the surrounding in-situ 
materials with the neat cement slurry grout as the drill bit is slowly rotated and withdrawn to 
form a relatively homogenous soil-cement column. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 39 
Hawaii • California 
In order to mitigate the potentially liquefiable area in the northern portion of 
Wharf B21, we recommend that overlapping jet grouted columns arranged in a grid 
pattern be installed to encase the problematic soils as shown on the Jet Grout Cell Layout, 
Plate 10. The jet-grouted columns would derive vertical support primarily from skin friction 
along the sides of the jet-grouted columns and/or from bearing on the medium stiff/dense 
soils. The jet grouted grid pattern would confine the liquefiable soils to provide ground 
reinforcement to mitigate liquefaction potential. Based on our evaluation of the subsurface 
conditions, we recommend using a 3-foot diameter jet-grouted column spaced at about 
2.5 feet on-center in 12.5 feet by 12.5 feet of closed cell grid pattern in general. Based on 
this configuration, we believe there will be a significant increase in the unconfined 
compressive strength of cementitious material with time. 
Items of the jet-grouted column foundations that are addressed in the succeeding 
subsections include the following: 
1. 
Jet-Grouted Column Foundations 
2. 
Jet-Grouting Equipment 
3. 
Jet-Grouting Test Program 
4. 
Quality Control 
5. 
Construction Considerations 
3.5.1 Jet-Grouted Column Foundations 
Based on our experience, the jet-grouted columns should have a minimum 
average diameter of 3 feet.  Due to the nature of jet-grouting, deviations from the 
specified minimum average diameter of the jet-grout column is anticipated 
depending on the subsurface conditions.  However, the jet-grout column should 
not have a diameter of less than 2.5 feet.  In addition, the grout mix should have a 
specific gravity of at least 1.6 and should be able to produce jet-grouted columns 
with a 7-day unconfined compressive strength of at least 200 pounds per square 
inch (psi) and a 28-day unconfined compressive strength of at least 400 psi. 
As noted previously, the 3-foot diameter jet-grouted columns should be installed in 
12.5 feet by 12.5 feet of closed cell grid configuration at about 2.5-foot 
center-to-center spacing as shown on the Jet Grout Cell Layout, Plate 10. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 40 
Hawaii • California 
In general, the jet-grouted column foundations should extend from about +105 feet 
USND to about +75 to +50 feet USND. Each jet-grouted column should be extended 
until relatively stiff/dense materials are encountered at each jet-grouted column 
location. It should be noted that the subsurface conditions at the site are highly 
variable. Therefore, we recommend that a probing program be conducted prior to the 
installation of production jet columns to determine the approximate depths of the 
proposed jet-grouted columns. The jet-grouted areas may be refined based on the 
results from the test probe program.  The recommended locations of the test probes 
are presented on Plate 10. 
Because the depths of each column foundation will vary depending on the 
subsurface conditions and the depth to the bottom of the lagoonal deposits, a 
Geolabs representative should be present to observe the jet-grouting operations 
during construction. 
3.5.2 Jet-Grouting Equipment 
Based on the subsurface conditions anticipated at the site, we recommend using 
the single fluid method of jet-grouting for mitigating the potentially liquefiable 
material for this project. The drilling equipment should be capable of advancing the 
jetting rods to the depth required. The drilling equipment also should be equipped 
with automated controls necessary to slowly rotate and withdraw the jetting rods 
at those rates determined necessary for the formation of the jet-grouted column 
foundations. Rates of rotation and withdrawal of the jetting rods for each column 
foundation should be recorded by the contractor and confirmed by a Geolabs 
representative. 
Grout mixers, holding tanks, and associated equipment should be capable of 
continuously producing a uniform grout mixture required for the formation of the 
jet-grouted column foundations. Uniformity of the grout mixture should be 
measured and recorded by the contractor by taking unit weight (density) 
measurements of the mixed grout by mud balance at least once every 
2,000 gallons of grout mixed and pumped. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 41 
Hawaii • California 
High-pressure pumps for jet-grouting operations should be capable of delivering 
grout at a minimum pressure of 4,000 psi. The high-pressure pumps should be 
equipped with the necessary gauges to measure and record grout pumping 
pressures, flow rate, and total grout used for each column foundation. 
3.5.3 Jet-Grouting Test Program 
We recommend implementing a jet-grouting test program to evaluate the proposed 
grouting methods and the ability of the proposed grout mix to produce jet-grouted 
column foundations meeting the depth, diameter, and material property 
requirements. The test program should be conducted and evaluated, including the 
results of 28-day unconfined compressive strength tests, prior to starting 
production jet-grouting work. 
To achieve these objectives, we recommend constructing at least one test section 
consisting of a minimum of four jet-grout columns using the same procedures 
proposed for the production jet-grouting work. The jet-grout columns should extend 
from about +107 feet USND to about +50 feet USND. The test columns should be 
installed to allow for later excavation below the groundwater level for physical 
inspection. Excavation to expose the grout columns of the test section should not 
be sooner than 7 days after the jet-grout columns have been constructed. 
After the jet-grout test columns have set up sufficiently, at least nine continuous 
core samples should be obtained from the full depth of the test columns. In general, 
we recommend employing triple tube core barrels with thin walls to obtain 
continuous core samples of the jet-grout columns. The core barrel should have a 
nominal inside diameter of at least 3 inches or greater. The core sample should be 
obtained from the locations shown on the Test Section Layout, Plate 11. 
The core samples should be inspected and checked for segregation. A minimum 
of four compression tests should be performed on each of the core samples in 
accordance with ASTM D1633 or ASTM D2850, as appropriate.  
We recommend a Geolabs representative be present during the jet-grouting test 
program to observe and evaluate the field performance of the proposed 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 42 
Hawaii • California 
jet-grouting equipment and methods. Therefore, observation of the jet-grouting 
operations by Geolabs is necessary and should be designated as a “Special 
Inspection” item. 
3.5.4 Quality Control 
The type of jet-grouting system and grouting parameters for grout mix, grout 
pressures, rotational speed, lifting rate, grout flow rate, number and size of jet 
nozzles, and drilling methods greatly affect the performance of the jet-grouted 
columns. Therefore, an adequate quality control program should be implemented 
during the production of jet-grouting operations. 
In general, grout mix uniformity should be verified by unit weight (density) 
measurements of the mixed grout by mud balance, Marsh Viscosity, and/or bleed 
from samples taken from the grout return line, in accordance with API Standard 13B 
test method. At least one group of tests should be conducted for every 2 hours that 
the grout is mixed and pumped. 
A minimum of six cement grout samples should be fabricated for every 
three production columns in accordance with ASTM C109. Two samples should be 
subjected to compressive strength tests at 7 days in accordance with ASTM C39 or 
C109 and ASTM D1633, respectively.  The remaining samples should be subjected 
to compressive strength tests at 28 days following the ASTM testing procedures. 
In addition, core samples should be taken after the production jet grouted columns 
have reached sufficient strength. We recommend that the vertical core samples be 
taken from the full depth of the treated columns of about 3 percent of the total number 
of jet-grouted columns. The core samples at each location should be tested for 
unconfined compressive strength as described in the “Jet Grouting Test Program” 
subsection. If the samples tested do not meet the specified strength requirements, 
then additional replacement jet grout columns may be required, or other provisions 
should be implemented, to compensate for the lower strength columns. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 43 
Hawaii • California 
3.5.5 Construction Considerations 
Existing underground utilities, including tieback dean-man anchors, are anticipated 
within the approximate jet-grouting limits. The contractor should be responsible for 
locating and avoiding the underground utility lines during the jet-grouted column 
foundation installation. 
In addition, grout, soil, and water spoil returns produced during the jet grouting 
operations should be contained and disposed of properly by the contractor. 
The subsurface conditions generally consist of loose/soft lagoonal deposits 
overlying dense coralline material and interbedded layers of stiff alluvium and 
weathered volcanic tuff.  It should be noted that cobbles and boulders may be 
present in the surface fill materials at the site.  In addition, boulders, cobbles and 
gravel materials are commonly encountered in the lagoonal deposits at the project 
site. Therefore, potentially difficult drilling conditions may be encountered and 
should be expected by the contractor.  The jet grouting contractor will need to have 
the appropriate equipment and tools to drill through these obstructions, where 
encountered. 
3.6 
Sheet Pile Bulkhead 
The following general guidelines may be used for design of the sheet pile bulkhead 
at the project site. 
3.6.1 Static Lateral Earth Pressures 
The sheet pile bulkhead should be designed to resist the lateral earth pressures 
due to the adjacent soils and surcharge effects. Considering restrained sheet pile 
bulkhead by the dead-man anchors, we believe that the following at-rest lateral 
earth pressures may be considered for the preliminary design of the sheet pile 
bulkhead, expressed as an equivalent fluid pressure in pounds per square foot per 
foot of depth (pcf). It should be noted that the lateral earth pressures provided 
above do not include hydrostatic pressures due to groundwater.

Surcharge stresses due to areal surcharges, line loads, and point loads within a 
horizontal distance equal to the depth of the sheet pile bulkhead should be 
considered in the design. For uniform surcharge stresses imposed on the loaded 
side of the bulkhead at Wharves B20/B21 (Zone-1) and B21 (Zone-2), a 
rectangular distribution with uniform pressures equal to 44 and 28 percent of the 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10

Page 45 
Hawaii • California 
vertical surcharge pressures acting on the vertical sheet pile up to about 80 feet 
USND, respectively, may be used in the design. Additional analyses during design 
may be needed to evaluate the surcharge effects of point loads and line loads. 
For waterfront structures such as sheet pile bulkheads, the maximum lateral 
pressures may be expected when the tide is at its lowest level. A receding tide may 
cause higher water levels behind the sheet pile than in front of it, depending on the 
type of soils behind the sheet piles. The high water level behind the sheet pile 
bulkhead will introduce additional seepage pressures due to hydrostatic load. For 
the type of soils encountered at the project site, an estimated hydrostatic pressure 
based on the maximum tide difference may be considered for the sheet pile design.

in the design of retaining structures. The force due to dynamic lateral earth 
pressures associated with seismic loading (PGA = 0.250g) may be estimated using 
the forces indicated in the table below for various allowable wall movements.

3.7 
Pavement Design 
We envision that new concrete pavements bearing on the recompacted on-site fill 
soils would be constructed adjacent to the wharves to improve crane access. We 
anticipate that the concrete pavement would be designed as an approach slab to support 
vehicles with a maximum single wheel load of 29 kips or a single-axle forklift with 49-kip 
load capacity. We have made our preliminary pavement designs based on the following 
design manuals and assumptions. 
DM-05.04, Civil Engineering – Pavements (October 1979). 
PCA Association, Thickness Design for Concrete Pavement (1966). 
UFC 3-320-06A, Concrete Floor Slabs on Grade Subjected to Heavy Loads 
(March 2005). 
Assumed 100,000 vehicle passes over the PCC pavement life 
Assumed modulus of subgrade reaction (k) to be 75 pci. 
Concrete flexural strength of 650 psi. 
Based on the above assumptions, we believe that the following pavement sections 
may be used for design purposes: 
Portland Cement Concrete Pavement for Vehicles with 29-kip Single Wheel Load 
  9.0-Inch Portland Cement Concrete 
12.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
21.0-Inch Total Pavement Thickness on Compacted Moist Subgrade 
Portland Cement Concrete Pavement for 49-Kip Capacity Single-Axle Forklift  
14.0-Inch Portland Cement Concrete 
12.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
26.0-Inch Total Pavement Thickness on Compacted Moist Subgrade 
The pavement subgrade soils should be scarified to a depth of at least 12 inches, 
moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, and compacted to at least 
95 percent relative compaction. The aggregate base and subbase courses also should 
be compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. The recommended 
pavement section assumes that good drainage will be provided for areas adjacent to the 
pavements.

Page 47 
Hawaii • California 
3.8 
Underground Utility Lines 
We envision some new utility lines and/or utility connections may be required for 
the project. We envision most of the new utility lines and/or utility connections for the 
project will be relatively shallow (within 5 to 8 feet of the ground surface). 
We anticipate most of the trenches for utilities will be excavated in the fills, 
soft/loose lagoonal deposits, and/or weathered volcanic tuff rock formation. In general, 
we recommend using granular bedding consisting of 6 inches of free-draining granular 
materials (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) below the pipes for uniform support. 
Free-draining granular materials, such as No. 3B Fine gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 
gradation), also should be used for the initial trench backfill up to about 12 inches above 
the pipes. 
It is critical to use this free-draining material to reduce the potential for the formation 
of voids below the haunches of the pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides 
of the pipes. Improper backfill material around the pipes and improper placement of the 
backfill could result in backfill settlement and pipe damage. Where groundwater is 
encountered, the bedding should be wrapped on all sides by non-woven filter fabric 
(Mirafi 180N or equivalent). 
In the event that soft/loose materials are encountered at the bottom of the trench 
invert, we recommend providing a subgrade stabilization layer consisting of 24 to 
30 inches of No. 2 Rock (ASTM C33, No. 4 gradation) wrapped in a non-woven filter 
fabric (Mirafi 180N or equivalent) below the bedding layer for uniform support. The 
stabilization layer should extend beyond the sides of the pipe a minimum width of 
one-fourth the outside diameter of the pipe or 12 inches, whichever is greater. 
The upper portion of the trench backfill from a level of 12 inches above the pipes 
to the top of the subgrade or finished grade may consist of the excavated granular 
materials with a maximum particle size of 6 inches or select granular fill materials. The 
backfill material should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, 
placed in maximum 8-inch level loose lifts, and mechanically compacted to at least 
90 percent relative compaction. In areas where trenches will be in paved areas, the upper 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8092-10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 48 
Hawaii • California 
3 feet of the trench backfill below the pavement finished grade should be compacted to 
no less than 95 percent relative compaction. 
3.9 
Design Review 
Preliminary and final drawings and specifications for the proposed facility 
improvements should be forwarded to Geolabs for review and written comments prior to 
construction. This review is needed to evaluate the conformance of the plans and 
specifications with the intent of the foundation recommendations provided herein. If this 
review is not made, Geolabs cannot assume responsibility for misinterpretation of our 
recommendations. 
3.10 Post-Design Services/Services During Construction 
Geolabs should be retained to provide geotechnical engineering services during 
construction of the proposed project. The critical items of construction monitoring that 
require "Special Inspection" include the following: 
Review of pile driving equipment and performance of a wave equation 
analysis 
Review of foundation installation submittals 
Observation of the test pile and the trial shaft programs 
Observation of the production pile driving operations and the drilled shaft 
foundation installation 
Observation of the jet-grouting test section 
Observation of the production jet-grout columns 
A Geolabs representative should monitor other aspects of earthwork construction 
to observe compliance with the intent of the design concepts, specifications, or 
recommendations and to expedite suggestions for design changes that may be required 
in the event that subsurface conditions differ from those anticipated at the time this report 
was prepared. The recommendations provided herein are contingent upon such 
observations.

The qualified geotechnical engineer should be a licensed civil engineer in the State

experience in similar subsurface conditions. The field observations should be performed 
by the geotechnical engineer or a representative from his/her firm under the direct 
supervision of the geotechnical engineer. If the actual exposed subsurface conditions 
encountered during construction are different from those encountered in our borings and 
considered in this report, then appropriate modifications to the design should be made.
